<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Gamergate controversy</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"GamerGate" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Gamergate_(disambiguation)" title="Gamergate (disambiguation)">Gamergate (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p>The <b>Gamergate controversy</b> concerns issues of <a href="/wiki/Sexism_in_video_gaming" title="Sexism in video gaming">sexism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">progressivism</a> in video game culture, stemming from a harassment campaign conducted primarily through the use of the <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> <a href="/wiki/Hashtag" title="Hashtag">hashtag</a> #GamerGate. <i>Gamergate</i> is used as a blanket term for the controversy, the harassment campaign and actions of those participating in it, and the loosely organized movement that emerged from the hashtag.</p>
<p>Beginning in August 2014, Gamergate targeted several women in the <a href="/wiki/Video_game_industry" title="Video game industry">video game industry</a>, including game developers <a href="/wiki/Zo%C3%AB_Quinn" title="Zoë Quinn">Zoë Quinn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brianna_Wu" title="Brianna Wu">Brianna Wu</a>, as well as feminist media critic <a href="/wiki/Anita_Sarkeesian" title="Anita Sarkeesian">Anita Sarkeesian</a>. After a former boyfriend of Quinn wrote a lengthy disparaging blog post about her, other people falsely accused her of entering a relationship with a journalist in exchange for positive coverage and threatened her with assault and murder. Those endorsing the blog post and spreading such accusations against Quinn organized themselves under the Twitter hashtag #Gamergate, as well as on <a href="/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat" title="Internet Relay Chat">Internet Relay Chat</a> (IRC) channels and websites such as <a href="/wiki/Reddit" title="Reddit">Reddit</a>, <a href="/wiki/4chan" title="4chan">4chan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/8chan" title="8chan">8chan</a>. Harassment campaigns against Quinn and others were coordinated through these forums and included <a href="/wiki/Doxing" title="Doxing">doxing</a>, threats of rape, and death threats. Many of those organizing under the Gamergate hashtag argue that they are campaigning against <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">political correctness</a> and poor <a href="/wiki/Journalism_ethics_and_standards" title="Journalism ethics and standards">journalistic ethics</a> in the video game industry, while numerous commentators have dismissed Gamergate's purported concerns with ethics and condemned its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Misogynistic" title="Misogynistic">misogynistic</a> behavior.</p>
<p>Most Gamergate supporters are anonymous, and the Gamergate movement has no official leaders, spokespeople, or manifesto. Statements claiming to represent Gamergate have been inconsistent and contradictory, making it difficult for commentators to identify goals and motives. As a result, Gamergate has often been defined by the harassment its supporters have committed. Some Gamergate supporters have attempted to dissociate themselves from misogyny and harassment, but their attempts have often been dismissed as insincere and self-serving.</p>
<p>The controversy has been described as a manifestation of a <a href="/wiki/Culture_war" title="Culture war">culture war</a> over cultural diversification, artistic recognition, and social criticism in video games, and over the social identity of gamers. Many supporters of Gamergate oppose what they view as the increasing influence of <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a> on video game culture. As a result, Gamergate is often viewed as a right-wing backlash against progressivism. Gamergate supporters claim to perceive collusion between the press and feminists, progressives, and social critics. These concerns have been dismissed by commentators as trivial, conspiracy theories, groundless, or unrelated to actual issues of ethics. Such concerns led users of the hashtag to launch email campaigns targeting firms advertising in publications of which they disapproved, asking them to withdraw their advertisements.</p>
<p>Industry responses to Gamergate have been predominantly negative. The <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Software_Association" title="Entertainment Software Association">Entertainment Software Association</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sony_Computer_Entertainment" title="Sony Computer Entertainment">Sony Computer Entertainment</a> have condemned Gamergate harassment. <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a>, which temporarily withdrew its advertisements from gaming news site <i><a href="/wiki/Gamasutra" title="Gamasutra">Gamasutra</a></i> as the result of a Gamergate email campaign, later pledged $300 million to support a "Diversity in Technology" program.</p>
<p>Gamergate has led figures both inside and outside the industry to focus more on better methods of tackling online harassment. U.S. Representative <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Clark" title="Katherine Clark">Katherine Clark</a> from Massachusetts has campaigned for a stronger government response to online harassment, gaining the support of Congress. Within the industry, organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Crash_Override_Network" title="Crash Override Network">Crash Override Network</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Online_Abuse_Prevention_Initiative" title="Online Abuse Prevention Initiative">Online Abuse Prevention Initiative</a> have been founded to provide support to those facing online harassment.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>In February 2013, <a href="/wiki/Zo%C3%AB_Quinn" title="Zoë Quinn">Zoë Quinn</a>, an independent game developer, released <i><a href="/wiki/Depression_Quest" title="Depression Quest">Depression Quest</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Interactive_fiction" title="Interactive fiction">interactive fiction</a> <a href="/wiki/Browser_game" title="Browser game">browser game</a>. The game was met with positive reviews in the gaming media, but some backlash developed among those who believed that it had received undue attention. Quinn began to receive <a href="/wiki/Hate_mail" title="Hate mail">hate mail</a> upon its release, leading her to change her phone number and screen her calls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcgamerdq_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-pcgamerdq-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmonitor_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardianDec3_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuardianDec3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KernelMag_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-KernelMag-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dot_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-dot-5">[5]</a></sup> By August 2014 Quinn had been the target of eighteen months of increasing harassment, which had created what <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> characterized as "an ambient hum of menace in her life, albeit one that she [had] mostly been able to ignore".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_yorker_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-new_yorker-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg/220px-Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg/330px-Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg/440px-Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zoe_Quinn_Car_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Game developer <a href="/wiki/Zo%C3%AB_Quinn" title="Zoë Quinn">Zoë Quinn</a>, the initial target of the harassment campaign</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In August 2014, Eron Gjoni, Quinn's former boyfriend, published the "Zoe Post", a 9,425-word blog post that quoted from personal chat logs, emails, and text messages to describe their relationship. The post, described as "a rambling online essay" in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_usu_threat_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_usu_threat-7">[7]</a></sup> complained, among other things, that Quinn entered a romantic relationship with Nathan Grayson, a journalist for the Gawker Media video game website <i>Kotaku</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldman_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldman-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeleStuart_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeleStuart-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYMag_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYMag-10">[10]</a></sup> The post was linked on 4chan, where some erroneously claimed the relationship had induced Grayson to publish a favorable review of <i>Depression Quest</i>. Grayson had never reviewed Quinn's games and Grayson's only article for <i>Kotaku</i> mentioning her was published before their relationship began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KotakuRefuted_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-KotakuRefuted-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcmag_about_gg_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-pcmag_about_gg-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker2-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canadacom_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-canadacom-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bostonmag_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-bostonmag-15">[15]</a></sup> Gjoni later updated his blog post to acknowledge this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcmag_about_gg_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-pcmag_about_gg-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Gjoni's blog post, Quinn and her family were subjected to a virulent and often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Misogynistic" title="Misogynistic">misogynistic</a> harassment campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nybooks_2015-06-25_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nybooks_2015-06-25-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup> The people behind this campaign initially referred to it as the "quinnspiracy", but adopted the Twitter <a href="/wiki/Hashtag" title="Hashtag">hashtag</a> "Gamergate" after it was coined by actor <a href="/wiki/Adam_Baldwin" title="Adam Baldwin">Adam Baldwin</a> near the end of August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chess_2015_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chess_2015-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobe_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobe-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CathyYoung_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-CathyYoung-21">[21]</a></sup> Baldwin has described Gamergate as a backlash against <a href="/wiki/Political_correctness" title="Political correctness">political correctness</a>, saying it has started a discussion "about culture, about ethics, and about freedom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spectator_2015-08-29_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-spectator_2015-08-29-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScaJo_movie_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScaJo_movie-23">[23]</a></sup> Journalists who did not cover the examination into Quinn's private life were accused of conspiracy, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blacklist" title="Blacklist">blacklist</a> circulated by Gamergate supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ibtimes1_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ibtimes1-24">[24]</a></sup> The accusations and harassment were coordinated by <a href="/wiki/4chan" title="4chan">4chan</a> users over <a href="/wiki/Internet_Relay_Chat" title="Internet Relay Chat">Internet Relay Chat</a> (IRC), spreading rapidly over <a href="/wiki/Imageboard" title="Imageboard">imageboards</a> and forums like 4chan and <a href="/wiki/Reddit" title="Reddit">Reddit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeleStuart_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-TeleStuart-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massanari2015_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Massanari2015-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ars_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ars-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Commentators both inside and outside the video game industry condemned the attacks against Quinn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dot_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-dot-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BostonGlobe_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-BostonGlobe-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBMarcotte_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-DBMarcotte-27">[27]</a></sup> The attacks included <a href="/wiki/Doxing" title="Doxing">doxing</a> (researching and broadcasting personally identifiable information about an individual) and hacking of her <a href="/wiki/Tumblr" title="Tumblr">Tumblr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dropbox_(service)" title="Dropbox (service)">Dropbox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Skype" title="Skype">Skype</a> accounts; she was also subjected to rape and death threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmonitor_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dot_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-dot-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBMarcotte_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-DBMarcotte-27">[27]</a></sup> The release of personal information forced Quinn to flee her home; she explained that "I can't go home because they have been posting around my home address, often with threats attached to it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>At a conference Quinn said, "I used to go to game events and feel like I was going home [...] Now it's just like... are any of the people I'm currently in the room with ones that said they wanted to beat me to death?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCQuinnInterview_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCQuinnInterview-29">[29]</a></sup> One such threat, reported in <i>The New Yorker</i>, proposed that: "Next time she shows up at a conference we... give her a crippling injury that's never going to fully heal... a good solid injury to the knees. I'd say a brain damage, but we don't want to make it so she ends up too retarded to fear us."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_yorker_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-new_yorker-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_harassment">Further harassment</span></h3>
<p>Gamergate supporters subjected others to similar harassment, doxing, and death threats. Those who came to the victims' defense were ridiculed as "white knights", or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_justice_warriors" title="Social justice warriors">social justice warriors</a>" (SJW);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup> this characterization was intended, according to Heron, Belford and Goker, to neutralize any opposition by questioning their motives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup> Shortly after the Gamergate hashtag was coined, video game developer <a href="/wiki/Phil_Fish" title="Phil Fish">Phil Fish</a> had his personal information, including various accounts and passwords, hacked and publicly posted in retaliation for defending Quinn and attacking her detractors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FishPaste_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FishPaste-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BI_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-BI-31">[31]</a></sup> The hacks and doxing also exposed documents relating to Fish's company, Polytron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DailyFish_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-DailyFish-32">[32]</a></sup> As a result, Fish left the gaming industry and put Polytron up for sale, calling the situation "unacceptable" and saying, "it's not worth it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmonitor_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FishPaste_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FishPaste-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs_fish_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-gs_fish-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg/220px-Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg/330px-Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg/440px-Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anita_Sarkeesian_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Feminist and media critic <a href="/wiki/Anita_Sarkeesian" title="Anita Sarkeesian">Anita Sarkeesian</a> faced death threats after releasing a <i><a href="/wiki/Tropes_vs._Women_in_Video_Games" title="Tropes vs. Women in Video Games">Tropes vs. Women in Video Games</a></i> video.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The campaign expanded to include renewed harassment of prominent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prominent_1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-prominent_1-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prominent_2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-prominent_2-35">[35]</a></sup> feminist media critic <a href="/wiki/Anita_Sarkeesian" title="Anita Sarkeesian">Anita Sarkeesian</a>, who had previously been a target of online harassment in part due to her YouTube video series <i><a href="/wiki/Tropes_vs._Women_in_Video_Games" title="Tropes vs. Women in Video Games">Tropes vs. Women in Video Games</a></i>, which analyzes sexist stereotypes in video games. Sarkeesian's attackers took her critical commentary as unfair and unwarranted, and considered her an interloper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheStar_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheStar-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TorontoExclusive_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-TorontoExclusive-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmo_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosmo-38">[38]</a></sup> After a new episode of <i>Tropes vs. Women</i> was released on August 24, 2014, Sarkeesian received rape and death threats, and private information including her home address was leaked; she was compelled to flee her home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BusinessWeekSarkessian_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-BusinessWeekSarkessian-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPostSarkeesian_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-WashPostSarkeesian-40">[40]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/XOXO_(festival)" title="XOXO (festival)">XOXO</a> arts and technology conference in Portland, Oregon, she said, in regard to the accusations that high-profile women were making up the threats against them, that "one of the most radical things you can do is to actually believe women when they talk about their experiences". "The perpetrators", Sarkeesian went on to say, "do not see themselves as perpetrators at all... They see themselves as noble warriors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VergeXOXO_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-VergeXOXO-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sarkeesian canceled an October 2014 speaking appearance at <a href="/wiki/Utah_State_University" title="Utah State University">Utah State University</a> (USU) after the school received three anonymous threats, the second of which claimed affiliation with Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SLT_ALberty101614_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-SLT_ALberty101614-42">[42]</a></sup> The initial threat proposed that "a Montreal Massacre style attack will be carried out against the attendees, as well as the students and staff at the nearby Women's Center", alluding to the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Polytechnique_massacre" title="École Polytechnique massacre">École Polytechnique massacre</a>, a 1989 mass shooting motivated by <a href="/wiki/Antifeminism" title="Antifeminism">antifeminism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-montreal-massacre-threat-ctv_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-montreal-massacre-threat-ctv-43">[43]</a></sup> USU's President and Provost released a joint statement saying that USU, in consultation with state and federal law enforcement agencies, had assessed that there was no credible threat to students, staff or the speaker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USUthreat_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-USUthreat-44">[44]</a></sup> Requests for additional security measures were declined because of Utah's <a href="/wiki/Open_carry_in_the_United_States" title="Open carry in the United States">open carry</a> laws, leading to the cancellation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_usu_threat_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_usu_threat-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnitaUSUCNN_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnitaUSUCNN-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnitaUSUBBC_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnitaUSUBBC-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnitaUSUGuardian_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-AnitaUSUGuardian-47">[47]</a></sup> The threats drew the attention of mainstream media to the Gamergate situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venturebeat_mainstream_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-venturebeat_mainstream-48">[48]</a></sup> Wingfield of <i>The New York Times</i> referred to the threat as "the most noxious example of a weeks long campaign to discredit or intimidate outspoken critics of the male-dominated gaming industry and its culture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_usu_threat_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_usu_threat-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI) investigated the threat to attack Sarkeesian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HJFBI_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-HJFBI-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4256" data-file-width="2832" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg/220px-Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg/330px-Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg/440px-Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brianna_Wu_next_to_Motorcycle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Video game developer <a href="/wiki/Brianna_Wu" title="Brianna Wu">Brianna Wu</a> suffered Gamergate-related harassment beginning in late 2014.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In mid-October <a href="/wiki/Brianna_Wu" title="Brianna Wu">Brianna Wu</a>, another independent game developer and co-founder of video game studio <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_Spacekat_(company)" title="Giant Spacekat (company)">Giant Spacekat</a>, saw her home address and other identifying information posted on 8chan as retaliation for mocking Gamergate. Wu then became the target of rape and death threats on Twitter and elsewhere. After contacting police, Wu fled her home with her husband, saying she would not allow the threats to intimidate her into silence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_usu_threat_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_usu_threat-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WuBoston_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-WuBoston-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WuGuardian_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-WuGuardian-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WuVenture_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-WuVenture-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polygon_11_October_2014_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_11_October_2014-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlobeFeature_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlobeFeature-54">[54]</a></sup> Wu later announced an <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$11,000</span> reward for any information leading to a conviction for those involved in her harassment, and set up a legal fund to help other game developers who have been harassed online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wu_reward_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-wu_reward-55">[55]</a></sup> As of April 2016, Wu was still receiving threats in such volume that she employed full-time staff to document them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csm_20160411_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-csm_20160411-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Harassment related to Gamergate continued for several months after the onset of the controversy. Two critics of Gamergate were targets of attempted "<a href="/wiki/Swatting" title="Swatting">swatting</a>"—hoaxed reports to emergency services intended to provoke a <a href="/wiki/SWAT" title="SWAT">SWAT</a> team response at the target's home. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported that both swatting attempts were coordinated through the "<a href="/wiki/Baphomet#Modern_interpretations_and_usage" title="Baphomet">baphomet</a>" subforum of 8chan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars_swatting_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars_swatting-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_swatting_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_swatting-58">[58]</a></sup> Since the initial rush of threats that caused her to flee her home, Wu documented receiving roughly 45 death threats by April 2015; Silicon Valley investor <a href="/wiki/Marc_Andreessen" title="Marc Andreessen">Marc Andreessen</a> has offered up to a $10,000 reward for information leading to the conviction of those who have issued these threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inc_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-inc-59">[59]</a></sup> Wu's studio, Giant Spacekat, withdrew from the Expo Hall of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penny_Arcade_Expo" title="Penny Arcade Expo">PAX East 2015</a>. Wu cited security concerns, lack of confidence in the management and their failure to return calls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wu_pax_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-wu_pax-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venturebeat_2015-02-19_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-venturebeat_2015-02-19-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bostonglobe_2015-02-24_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-bostonglobe_2015-02-24-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Actress and gamer <a href="/wiki/Felicia_Day" title="Felicia Day">Felicia Day</a> wrote a blog post about her concerns over Gamergate and her fear of retaliation if she spoke against it. Almost immediately her home address and phone number were posted online, leading to harassing letters and phone calls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes_2015-09-21_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes_2015-09-21-63">[63]</a></sup> Actor <a href="/wiki/Wil_Wheaton" title="Wil Wheaton">Wil Wheaton</a> and former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NFL" title="NFL">NFL</a> player <a href="/wiki/Chris_Kluwe" title="Chris Kluwe">Chris Kluwe</a> also posted criticisms of Gamergate. Inasmuch as the latter is widely noted for his use of creative insults,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapost_felicia_day_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapost_felicia_day-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DayGuardian_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-DayGuardian-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DayTime_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-DayTime-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DayCNN_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-DayCNN-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Colbert" title="Stephen Colbert">Stephen Colbert</a> questioned why men like Kluwe had not been threatened by Gamergate, noting that "it's almost entirely women being threatened in Gamergate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColbertVerge_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColbertVerge-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPoColbert_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaPoColbert-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some people who have identified as supporters of Gamergate said that they have been harassed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diver_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsukayama_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsukayama-71">[71]</a></sup> YouTube personality <a href="/wiki/Boogie2988" title="Boogie2988">Steven "Boogie2988" Williams</a>, remarked that a comment on one of his videos included his address and a threat to his wife's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diver_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Codd_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Codd-72">[72]</a></sup> In an interview with BBC Three, Gamergate supporter <a href="/wiki/TotalBiscuit" title="TotalBiscuit">John Bain</a>, known by his YouTube moniker "TotalBiscuit," said he has been the target of death threats and harassment from anonymous people who opposed his view on Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc3_2016-03-16_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc3_2016-03-16-73">[73]</a></sup> Mike Diver wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/Vice_(magazine)" title="Vice (magazine)">Vice</a></i> that threats against Gamergate supporters had been neglected in press coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diver_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-70">[70]</a></sup> Misogynist abuse and vitriolic messages were targeted at many people involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_gg_condemned_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_gg_condemned-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bomb threats have also been made towards events attended by Gamergate supporters. A May 2015 meeting in Washington D.C. arranged by writer <a href="/wiki/Christina_Hoff_Sommers" title="Christina Hoff Sommers">Christina Hoff Sommers</a> and journalist <a href="/wiki/Milo_Yiannopoulos" title="Milo Yiannopoulos">Milo Yiannopoulos</a> was the target of a bomb threat made over Twitter, according to local police responding to information supplied by the FBI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon_2015-05-03_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon_2015-05-03-75">[75]</a></sup> During "Airplay", an event run by the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Professional_Journalists" title="Society of Professional Journalists">Society of Professional Journalists</a> in August 2015, multiple bomb threats were made. This led to the evacuation of the building and the surrounding neighborhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon_2015-08-16_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon_2015-08-16-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_of_harassment">Coordination of harassment</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Ars_Technica" title="Ars Technica">Ars Technica</a></i> reported that a series of 4chan discussion logs suggests that Twitter <a href="/wiki/Sockpuppet_(Internet)" title="Sockpuppet (Internet)">sockpuppet</a> accounts were used to popularize the Gamergate hashtag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ars_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ars-26">[26]</a></sup> Heron, Belford, and Goker, analyzing the logs, said that early Gamergate IRC discussions focused on coordinating the harassment of Quinn by using <a href="/wiki/Astroturfing" title="Astroturfing">astroturf</a> campaigns to push attacks against her into mainstream view. They also describe how initial organizers deliberately attempted to cultivate a palpable narrative for public consumption while internally focusing on personal grudges against Quinn and aggressive sexual imagery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup> Mortensen wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/Games_and_Culture" title="Games and Culture">Games and Culture</a></i> that Gamergate's structure as an anonymous swarm allowed it to create an environment where anyone who criticized it or became its target was at risk, while allowing them to avoid individual responsibility for harassment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortensen2016_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mortensen2016-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>There has been considerable discussion of <a href="/wiki/Norm_(social)" title="Norm (social)">self-policing</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Collective_responsibility" title="Collective responsibility">responsibility</a> supporters of Gamergate share when the hashtag is used for harassment. A number of websites have blocked users, removed posts, and created policies to prevent their users from threatening Quinn and others with doxing, assault, rape and murder, and planning and coordinating such threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmonitor_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dot_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-dot-5">[5]</a></sup> 4chan's founder, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Poole" title="Christopher Poole">Christopher Poole</a>, banned all discussion of Gamergate on the site as more attacks occurred, leading to Gamergate supporters using 8chan as their central hub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massanari2015_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Massanari2015-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapost_8chan_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapost_8chan-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gamergate supporters have responded to accusations of harassment in a variety of ways. Many have denied that the harassment came from Gamergate, or falsely accused victims of fabricating the evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPostSarkeesian_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-WashPostSarkeesian-40">[40]</a></sup> Gamergate supporters have used the term "Literally Who" to refer to victims of harassment such as Quinn, saying they are not relevant to Gamergate's goals and purposes. Commentators have decried the use of such terminology as dehumanizing, and said that discussions on Gamergate forums often center around those referred to as "Literally Who".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYMag_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYMag-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DipCour_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-DipCour-79">[79]</a></sup> Some Gamergate supporters have denounced the harassment, arguing that the perpetrators are in the minority and do not represent them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diver_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsukayama_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsukayama-71">[71]</a></sup> Gamergate supporters have also reported threatening or hateful comments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endgg_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-endgg-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>By September 24, 2014, over one million Twitter messages incorporating the Gamergate hashtag had been sent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metro_what_is_gg_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-metro_what_is_gg-81">[81]</a></sup> A <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Brandwatch" title="Brandwatch">Brandwatch</a> analysis found more than two million Twitter messages between September and October 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newsweek_Brandwatch_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newsweek_Brandwatch-82">[82]</a></sup> Software developer <a href="/wiki/Andy_Baio" title="Andy Baio">Andy Baio</a> also produced an analysis of #Gamergate tweets showing a discussion that was polarized between pro- and anti-Gamergate factions. One quarter of the tweets sampled were produced by users new to Twitter, most of whom were pro-Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastcodesign_2014-10-28_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-fastcodesign_2014-10-28-83">[83]</a></sup> While the number of Gamergate supporters is unclear, in October 2014 <i><a href="/wiki/Deadspin" title="Deadspin">Deadspin</a></i> estimated 10,000 supporters based on the number of users discussing Gamergate on Reddit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-columbia_journalism_review_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-columbia_journalism_review-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In an interview with NPR's <i><a href="/wiki/Marketplace_(radio_program)" title="Marketplace (radio program)">Marketplace</a></i>, voice actress <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Hale" title="Jennifer Hale">Jennifer Hale</a> called on the gaming community to improve the self-policing of its small and vicious fringe, and said race and gender barriers persist in the industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marketplace_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marketplace-85">[85]</a></sup> Developer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Molyneux" title="Peter Molyneux">Peter Molyneux</a> considered that the Internet's instant accessibility of social media allows for people to express of-the-moment opinions without thinking about their consequences, leading to a "whole Pandora's Box" of both good and bad issues that society must consider in terms of freedom of speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-develop_harassment_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-develop_harassment-86">[86]</a></sup> Todd VanDerWerff wrote that the Gamergate supporters' message was lost in the vitriolic harassment, frequently directed at women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_cultural_implications">Social and cultural implications</span></h2>
<p>Observers have generally described Gamergate as part of a long-running culture war against efforts to diversify the traditionally male video gaming community, particularly targeting outspoken women. They cite Gamergate supporters' frequent harassment of female figures in the gaming industry and its overt hostility toward people involved in social criticism and analysis of video games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-columbia_journalism_review_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-columbia_journalism_review-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstthings_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-firstthings-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DustinTurner2016_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-DustinTurner2016-90">[90]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Vox_(website)" title="Vox (website)">Vox</a></i> said that Gamergate supporters were less interested in criticizing ethical issues than in opposition to social criticism and analysis of video games and in harassment of prominent women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VoxLose_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-VoxLose-91">[91]</a></sup> <i>Ars Technica</i> quoted early members as saying that they had no interest in videogames and were primarily interested in attacking Quinn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ars_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ars-26">[26]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/First_Things" title="First Things">First Things</a></i>, Nathaniel Givens described Gamergate as a reaction to the aggressive promotion of a progressive environment in video game culture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstthings_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-firstthings-89">[89]</a></sup> while Carter Dotson blamed progressives themselves for the backlash, which he believed to be a reaction to their negative mode of engagement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pocketgamer_2015-10-08_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-pocketgamer_2015-10-08-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gamergate has been described as being driven by anti-feminist ideologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vice_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vice-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-right_wing_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-right_wing-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nordic_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-nordic-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bostonglobe_2015-06-12_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-bostonglobe_2015-06-12-96">[96]</a></sup> Some supporters have denied this, but acknowledge that there are misogynistic voices within Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYMag_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYMag-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diver_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsukayama_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tsukayama-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vice_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vice-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vergestop_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-vergestop-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jon_Stone_(poet)" title="Jon Stone (poet)">Jon Stone</a>, in <i>The Guardian</i>, called it a "swelling of vicious right-wing sentiment" and compared it to the <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_rights_movement" title="Men's rights movement">men's rights movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-right_wing_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-right_wing-94">[94]</a></sup> Commentators such as Jon Stone, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liana_Kerzner" title="Liana Kerzner">Liana Kerzner</a> and Ryan Cooper have said that the controversy is being exploited by right-wing voices and by conservative pundits who had little interest in gaming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-right_wing_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-right_wing-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetalEater2_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-MetalEater2-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryan_Cooper_Week_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ryan_Cooper_Week-99">[99]</a></sup> Chrisella Herzog states that in addition to violent sexism, Gamergate has virulent strains and violent sentiments of homophobia, transphobia, anti-Semitism, racism, and neo-Nazism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DipCour_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-DipCour-79">[79]</a></sup> Some in the gamergate movement went on to be part of the <a href="/wiki/Alt-right" title="Alt-right">alt-right</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alt-right_Component_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alt-right_Component-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Quinn said the campaign had "roped well-meaning people who cared about ethics and transparency into a pre-existing hate mob",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ViceQuinn_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-ViceQuinn-101">[101]</a></sup> and urged industry publishers and developers to condemn the hashtag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_gg_condemned_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_gg_condemned-74">[74]</a></sup> She further asked those Gamergate supporters who had any earnest discussion about ethics to move away from the "Gamergate" tag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_gg_condemned_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_gg_condemned-74">[74]</a></sup> In <i>Der Bund</i>, Jan Rothenberger wrote that a majority of gamers were distancing themselves from the hate campaigns, and that some supporters were seeking a new banner because "Gamergate" is now indelibly associated with such campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bund_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bund-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nathaniel Givens said that, regardless of their actions, Gamergate supporters were painted in a negative light due to associating themselves with Gamergate, which was now a toxic term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firstthings_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-firstthings-89">[89]</a></sup> Alex Goldman from <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Media" title="On the Media">On the Media</a></i> wrote that Gamergate's involvement in harassment had caused it to lose mainstream credibility, and advised its supporters to adopt a self-identifier other than gamer as a way of distancing themselves from their worst representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldman_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-goldman-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gamer_identity">Gamer identity</span></h3>
<p>The Gamergate situation is often considered to be a reaction to the changing cultural identity of the "<a href="/wiki/Gamer" title="Gamer">gamer</a>". The notion of a gamer identity emerged in video game magazines catering to the interests of an audience that was predominantly young and male. These publications were seen by industry leaders as a means of promoting their products, and the close relationship between gaming journalists and major gaming companies drew criticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardianKS_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuardianKS-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kubas-Meyer_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kubas-Meyer-104">[104]</a></sup> Over the years, the growing popularity of games expanded their audience to include many who did not fit the traditional gamer demographic. Games with artistic and cultural themes grew in popularity, and <a href="/wiki/Independent_video_game_development" title="Independent video game development">independent video game development</a> made these games more common, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_games" title="Mobile games">mobile</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Casual_games" title="Casual games">casual</a> games expanded the scope of the industry beyond the traditional gamer identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VoxConfuse_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-VoxConfuse-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetalEater1_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-MetalEater1-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShawChess2016_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShawChess2016-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2014 annual survey by the <a href="/wiki/Entertainment_Software_Association" title="Entertainment Software Association">Entertainment Software Association</a> showed that nearly as many women played video games (48%) as men,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamesindustry_women_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamesindustry_women-110">[110]</a></sup> and this broader audience began to question some assumptions and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fantasy_tropes_and_conventions" title="Fantasy tropes and conventions">tropes</a> that had been common in games. Shira Chess and Adrienne Shaw wrote that concern over these changes is integral to Gamergate, especially a fear that sexualized games aimed primarily at young men might eventually be replaced by less sexualized games marketed to broader audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chess_2015_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chess_2015-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics became interested in issues of <a href="/wiki/Gender_representation_in_video_games" title="Gender representation in video games">gender representation</a> and identity in video games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Time-106">[106]</a></sup> One prominent feminist critic of the representation of women in gaming is Anita Sarkeesian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prominent_1_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-prominent_1-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prominent_2_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-prominent_2-35">[35]</a></sup> whose <i>Tropes vs. Women in Video Games</i> project is devoted to female stereotypes in games. Her fund-raising campaign and videos were met with hostility and harassment by some gamers. Further incidents raised concerns about <a href="/wiki/Sexism_in_video_gaming" title="Sexism in video gaming">sexism in video gaming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-Time-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PolygonFBI_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-PolygonFBI-111">[111]</a></sup> Prior to August 2014, escalating harassment prompted the <a href="/wiki/International_Game_Developers_Association" title="International Game Developers Association">International Game Developers Association</a> (IGDA) to provide support groups for harassed developers and to begin discussions with the FBI to help investigate online harassment of game developers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PolygonFBI_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-PolygonFBI-111">[111]</a></sup> In an interview on <a href="/wiki/Comedy_Central" title="Comedy Central">Comedy Central</a>'s program <i><a href="/wiki/The_Colbert_Report" title="The Colbert Report">The Colbert Report</a></i>, Sarkeesian said she believes women are targeted because they are "challenging the status quo of gaming as a male-dominated space".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colbert_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colbert-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late August 2014, shortly after the initial accusations against Grayson and harassment of Quinn, several gaming sites published <a href="/wiki/Op-ed" title="Op-ed">op-eds</a> on the controversy focused on the growing diversity of gaming and the mainstreaming of the medium, some of which included criticism of sexism within gamer culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plunkett_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plunkett-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston-114">[114]</a></sup> The timing and number of articles published on or around August 28 was seen by Gamergate supporters as evidence of a conspiracy to declare the death of the gamer identity, according to Chess and Shaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chess_2015_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chess_2015-19">[19]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Slate_(magazine)" title="Slate (magazine)">Slate</a>'s</i> <a href="/wiki/David_Auerbach" title="David Auerbach">David Auerbach</a> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Sentinel_(Staffordshire)" title="The Sentinel (Staffordshire)">The Sentinel's</a></i> David Elks criticized these articles for alienating their publications' audience by attacking the gamer identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-slate-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stokesentinel_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-stokesentinel-116">[116]</a></sup> Writing for <i><a href="/wiki/Paste_(magazine)" title="Paste (magazine)">Paste</a></i>, L. Rhodes said the antagonism in the Gamergate controversy was a result of the industry seeking to widen its customer demographic instead of focusing on core gamers, which Rhodes says "is precisely what videogames needed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paste_2015-04-20_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-paste_2015-04-20-117">[117]</a></sup> Brendan Keogh of <i><a href="/wiki/Overland_(magazine)" title="Overland (magazine)">Overland</a></i> stated that Gamergate "does not represent a marginalised, discriminated identity under attack so much as a hegemonic and normative mainstream being forced to redistribute some of its power".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overland_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-overland-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Misogyny_and_sexism">Misogyny and sexism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sexism_in_video_gaming" title="Sexism in video gaming">Sexism in video gaming</a></div>
<p>Gamergate has been associated with sexism, misogyny, and criticism of both feminism and those it labels as "social justice warriors". According to Sarah Kaplan of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, "sexism in gaming is a long-documented, much-debated but seemingly intractable problem", and became the crux of the Gamergate controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo-16">[16]</a></sup> Jaime Weinman writing in <i><a href="/wiki/Maclean%27s" title="Maclean's">Maclean's</a></i> said, "[w]hether it was supposed to be or not, GamerGate is largely about women".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macleans_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macleans-119">[119]</a></sup> Discussing Gamergate on her <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a> blog, Jane McManus compared the misogyny that women in the gaming industry experience to that faced by the first women entering sporting communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESPNMcManus_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESPNMcManus-120">[120]</a></sup> Canadian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Justin_Trudeau" title="Justin Trudeau">Justin Trudeau</a> has described Gamergate as "something that we need to stand clearly against".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada.27s_feminist_Prime_Minister_Justin_Trudeau_targets_GamerGate_in_anti-misogyny_call_to_action_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada.27s_feminist_Prime_Minister_Justin_Trudeau_targets_GamerGate_in_anti-misogyny_call_to_action-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sexism and misogyny had been identified as problems in the video game industry and online community prior to the events of Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gdc_misogyny_2012_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-gdc_misogyny_2012-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women_remain_outsiders_in_video_game_industry_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Women_remain_outsiders_in_video_game_industry-123">[123]</a></sup> Sarkeesian considered that the Internet has a "boys'-locker-room feel" to it, with male users trying to show off to each other which causes escalating cases of harassment in situations like Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_aug2015_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_aug2015-124">[124]</a></sup> In March 2014, game designer <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Bleszinski" title="Cliff Bleszinski">Cliff Bleszinski</a> wrote a blog post commenting on the "latent racism, homophobia and misogyny" that existed within the online gaming community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTSuellentrop_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTSuellentrop-125">[125]</a></sup> In a November 2014 interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Develop_(magazine)" title="Develop (magazine)">Develop</a></i>, Wu said the game industry "has been a boys' club for 30 years", and that the common portrayal of women as "sex symbols and <a href="/wiki/Damsel_in_distress" title="Damsel in distress">damsels in distress</a>" in video games has led to the players taking the same attitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-develop_harassment_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-develop_harassment-86">[86]</a></sup> Brendan Sinclair, writing for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GamesIndustry.biz" title="GamesIndustry.biz">GamesIndustry.biz</a></i>, stated that the events of the Gamergate controversy were "reprehensible and saddening" and "this industry has some profound issues in the way it treats women".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gibiz_silver_lining_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-gibiz_silver_lining-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many commentators have said that the harassment associated with Gamergate springs from this existing well of deep-seated misogyny, and that it was merely brought to the fore by the anonymity of the Internet. Regarding the false allegations against Quinn, <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Marcotte" title="Amanda Marcotte">Amanda Marcotte</a> in an article for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Beast" title="The Daily Beast">The Daily Beast</a></i> accused the video game world of being "thick with misogynists who are aching to swarm" with hate on any "random woman held up for them to hate, no matter what the pretext". She related these attacks to harassment sent to a woman who criticized a <i><a href="/wiki/Teen_Titans" title="Teen Titans">Teen Titans</a></i> cover and to a community manager of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mighty_No._9" title="Mighty No. 9">Mighty No. 9</a></i> game because she drew a feminine <a href="/wiki/Mega_Man_(character)" title="Mega Man (character)">Mega Man</a>, and virtual rapes committed against women's player avatars in <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Theft_Auto_V" title="Grand Theft Auto V">Grand Theft Auto V</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/DayZ_(video_game)" title="DayZ (video game)">DayZ</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBMarcotte_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-DBMarcotte-27">[27]</a></sup> In an interview with the BBC, Quinn stated that "Before [Gamergate] had a name, it was nothing but trying to get me to kill myself, trying to get people to hurt me, going after my family. [...] There is no mention of ethics in journalism at all outside of making the same accusation everybody makes towards any successful woman; that clearly she got to where she is because she had sex with someone."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCQuinnInterview_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBCQuinnInterview-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Danielle_Citron" title="Danielle Citron">Danielle Citron</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland,_College_Park" title="University of Maryland, College Park">University of Maryland</a> wrote that the intent of this type of harassment is to demean the victim, make them doubt their own integrity, and to redefine the victim's identity in order to "fundamentally distort who she is".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inc_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-inc-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Targets of Gamergate supporters have overwhelmingly been women, even when men were responsible for the supposed wrongdoings. Writing in <i>The New Yorker</i>, Simon Parkin observed that Quinn was attacked while the male journalist who was falsely accused of reviewing her work favorably largely escaped, revealing the campaign as "a pretense to make further harassment of women in the industry permissible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-new_yorker_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-new_yorker-6">[6]</a></sup> In <i>The New York Times</i>, Chris Suellentrop said that a petition sought to have a female colleague fired for criticizing the portrayal of women in <i>Grand Theft Auto V</i>, while he and many other male critics raised similar concerns but did not face similar reprisals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTSuellentrop_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTSuellentrop-125">[125]</a></sup> Most commentators have described Gamergate as consisting largely of white males, though some supporters have said that the movement includes a notable percentage of women, minorities and LGBT members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inquisitr_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-inquisitr-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Week" title="The Week">The Week</a></i>, Ryan Cooper called the harassment campaign "an online form of terrorism" intended to reverse a trend in gaming culture toward increasing acceptance of women, and stated that social media platforms need to tighten their policies and protections against threats and abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheWeek_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheWeek-128">[128]</a></sup> Speaking on <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Public_Radio" title="Iowa Public Radio">Iowa Public Radio</a>, academic Cindy Tekobbe said the harassment campaign was intended to drive women from public spaces and intimidate them into silence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPR_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPR-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prof. Joanne St. Lewis of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Ottawa" title="University of Ottawa">University of Ottawa</a> stated that Gamergate's harassment and threats should be considered acts of terrorism as the perpetrators seek to harm women and to prevent them from speaking back or defending others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vice_2015-02-13_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-vice_2015-02-13-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_enforcement">Law enforcement</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Katherine_clark_june_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="389" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Katherine_clark_june_2014.jpg/220px-Katherine_clark_june_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Katherine_clark_june_2014.jpg/330px-Katherine_clark_june_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Katherine_clark_june_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Katherine_clark_june_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Katherine_Clark" title="Katherine Clark">Katherine Clark</a>, the U.S. Congresswoman from Massachusetts' 5th District, sought to expand the FBI's ability to take action against cyberharassment similar to that faced by Wu.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Though <i>Newsweek</i> reported that the FBI had a file regarding Gamergate, no arrests have been made nor charges filed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsweek_FBI_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek_FBI-131">[131]</a></sup> Former FBI supervisory special agent for cybercrimes, Tim Ryan, stated that cyberharassment cases are a low priority for authorities because it is difficult to track down the perpetrator and they have lower penalties compared to other crimes they are tasked to enforce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_2014-10-17_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-slate_2014-10-17-132">[132]</a></sup> In June 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">US Supreme Court</a> ruled in <i><a href="/wiki/Elonis_v._United_States" title="Elonis v. United States">Elonis v. United States</a></i> that harassing messages sent online are not necessarily <a href="/wiki/True_threat" title="True threat">true threats</a> that would be prosecutable under criminal law and, according to <i><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Standard" title="Pacific Standard">Pacific Standard</a></i>, this poses a further challenge in policing Gamergate-related harassment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psmag_2015-06-04_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-psmag_2015-06-04-133">[133]</a></sup> However, the Court's decision also suggested that if threats made over social media were found to be true threats, they should be treated the same as threats made in other forms of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastcompany_2015-06-02_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-fastcompany_2015-06-02-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wu has expressed her frustration over how law enforcement agencies have responded to the threats that she and other women in the game industry have received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marysue_2015-05-20_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-marysue_2015-05-20-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars_2015-05-21_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars_2015-05-21-136">[136]</a></sup> The lack of legal enforcement contributes towards the harassers' ability to maintain these activities without any risk of punishment, according to Chrisella Herzog of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Diplomatic_Courier" title="The Diplomatic Courier">The Diplomatic Courier</a></i>; at worst, harassers would see their social media accounts suspended but are able to turn around to register new accounts to continue to engage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DipCour_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-DipCour-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>U.S. Representative <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Clark" title="Katherine Clark">Katherine Clark</a> from Massachusetts wrote a letter to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_Appropriations_Committee" title="House Appropriations Committee">House Appropriations Committee</a> asking it to call on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> to crack down on the harassment of women on the internet, saying the campaign of intimidation associated with Gamergate had highlighted the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clark_2015-03-10_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-clark_2015-03-10-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge_2015-03-11_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge_2015-03-11-138">[138]</a></sup> Clark also hosted a Congressional briefing on March 15, 2015, along with the Congressional Victims' Rights Caucus to review issues of cyberstalking and online threats; during the briefing, Quinn spoke to her experience with Gamergate, which an executive director of the <a href="/wiki/National_Coalition_Against_Domestic_Violence" title="National Coalition Against Domestic Violence">National Coalition Against Domestic Violence</a> described during the hearing as "an online hate group [...] which was started by an ex-boyfriend to ruin [Quinn's] life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon_2015-04-21_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon_2015-04-21-139">[139]</a></sup> On May 27, 2015, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> formally supported Clark's request for increased measures to combat online abuse against women, explicitly pressing for more investigations and prosecutions by the Department of Justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wgbh_2015-06-01_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-wgbh_2015-06-01-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salon_2015-05-29_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salon_2015-05-29-141">[141]</a></sup> On June 2, 2015, Rep. Clark introduced H.R. 2602, the "Prioritizing Online Threat Enforcement Act of 2015" to Congress. The bill would allocate more funding for the FBI to employ additional agents to enforce laws against <a href="/wiki/Cyberstalking" title="Cyberstalking">cyberstalking</a>, online criminal harassment, and threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metrowest_2015-06-04_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-metrowest_2015-06-04-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.R.2602_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-H.R.2602-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo_2015-06-07_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo_2015-06-07-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate_over_ethics_allegations">Debate over ethics allegations</span></h2>
<p>Some Gamergate supporters contend that their actions are driven by concern for ethics in videogame journalism, arguing that the close relationships between journalists and developers provide evidence of an unethical conspiracy among reviewers to focus on progressive social issues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KernelMag_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-KernelMag-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYMag_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYMag-10">[10]</a></sup> leading to conflicts of interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC1_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC1-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Straumsheim2015_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Straumsheim2015-146">[146]</a></sup> As evidence of this, they point to what they consider as disproportionate praise that video game journalism has given broadly towards recent games such as <i><a href="/wiki/Depression_Quest" title="Depression Quest">Depression Quest</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Gone_Home" title="Gone Home">Gone Home</a></i>, which offer little conventional gameplay, require minimal skill to complete, and relate stories with social implications, while traditional <a href="/wiki/AAA_(video_game_industry)" title="AAA (video game industry)">AAA titles</a> are downplayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overland_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-overland-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTSuellentrop_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTSuellentrop-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-willametteweek_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-willametteweek-147">[147]</a></sup> Some commentators have argued that harassment and misogyny within Gamergate has prevented those with valid ethics concerns from being heard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup> Many of Gamergate's claims have been rejected as ill-founded and unsupported. <i>The Week</i>, <i>Vox</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(magazine)" title="Wired (magazine)">Wired</a></i>, among others, stated that discussions of gender equality, sexism and other social issues in game reviews present no ethical issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortensen2016_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mortensen2016-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VoxLose_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-VoxLose-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambinder_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ambinder-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WiredHudson_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-WiredHudson-149">[149]</a></sup> An analysis of a week's worth of public posts tagged with the hashtag said that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Issue_publics" title="Issue publics">issue publics</a> were not primarily about ethics in gaming journalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BurgessMatamoros-Fern.C3.A1ndez2016_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-BurgessMatamoros-Fern.C3.A1ndez2016-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-September 2014, <a href="/wiki/Milo_Yiannopoulos" title="Milo Yiannopoulos">Milo Yiannopoulos</a> published leaked discussions from a mailing list for gaming journalists called GameJournoPros on the <a href="/wiki/Breitbart_News" title="Breitbart News">Breitbart News</a> website, which included discussion of events related to Gamergate. Yiannopoulos and Gamergate supporters saw the mailing list as evidence of collusion among journalists, drawing comparisons between it and <a href="/wiki/JournoList" title="JournoList">JournoList</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsukayama_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tsukayama-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GGJargon_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-GGJargon-151">[151]</a></sup> In an interview with <i>Vice</i>, one Gamergate supporter said "GameJournoPros exemplifies every single major ethical problem with modern games journalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diver_70-5"><a href="#cite_note-Diver-70">[70]</a></sup> The list's founder acknowledged suggesting that journalists write an open letter of support to Quinn in response to the harassment she was facing, but said other members of the list had rejected his suggestion and helped him understand why his idea was inappropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GJPresponse_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-GJPresponse-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FudgeResponse_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FudgeResponse-153">[153]</a></sup> Commentators did not consider the list to represent collusion, observing that it is a standard practice across professions to adopt informal venues for discussing matters of professional interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-westmanjournal_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-westmanjournal-154">[154]</a></sup> Following the leak, the mailing list was closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FudgeResponse_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-FudgeResponse-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berkman_Center_for_Internet_%26_Society" title="Berkman Center for Internet &amp; Society">Berkman Center for Internet &amp; Society</a> at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> described Gamergate as a "vitriolic campaign against Quinn that quickly morph[ed] into a broader crusade against alleged corruption in games journalism" which involved considerable abuse and harassment of female developers and game critics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Internet_Monitor_2014_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Internet_Monitor_2014-155">[155]</a></sup> Other commentators argued that Gamergate had the potential to raise significant issues in gaming journalism, but that the wave of misogynistic harassment and abuse associated with the hashtag had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poisoned_the_well" title="Poisoned the well">poisoned the well</a>, making it impossible to separate honest criticism from sexist trolling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldman_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-goldman-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-87">[87]</a></sup> The authors of "Sexism in the Circuitry" argued that some Gamergate supporters were "genuinely motivated by a desire to uncover these issues and improve the quality of journalism, such as it is, within the game industry", but said that viable discussion was obscured by the harassment and misogyny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeronBelford2014_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-HeronBelford2014-18">[18]</a></sup> Concerns have also been raised when juxtaposing the behavior of Gamergate supporters with their claimed message: Dr. Kathleen Bartzen Culver, a professor and media ethics expert at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a>, wrote that while Gamergate supporters claimed to be interested in journalism ethics, their "misogynistic and threatening" behavior belied this claim. "Much of the conversation—if I can even call it that—has been a toxic sludge of rumor, invective, and gender bias. The irony comes from people who claim to be challenging the ethics of game journalists through patently unethical behavior."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UWiscCulver_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-UWiscCulver-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In an interview with Anita Sarkeesian in <i>The Guardian</i>, Jessica Valenti said that "the movement's much-mocked mantra, 'It's about ethics in journalism'" was seen by others as "a natural extension of sexist harassment and the fear of female encroachment on a traditionally male space". Sarkeesian asked, "if this 'movement' was about journalism, why wasn't it journalists who had to deal with a barrage of rape and death threats?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_aug2015_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian_aug2015-124">[124]</a></sup> Writing in <i>Vox</i>, Todd VanDerWerff said that "[e]very single question of journalistic ethics Gamergate has brought up has either been debunked or dealt with".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VoxLose_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-VoxLose-91">[91]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Leigh_Alexander_(journalist)" title="Leigh Alexander (journalist)">Leigh Alexander</a>, then editor-at-large of <i>Gamasutra</i>, described the ethics concerns as deeply sincere but based on <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theory" title="Conspiracy theory">conspiracy theories</a>, saying that there is nothing unethical about journalists being acquainted with those they cover and that meaningful reporting requires journalists to develop professional relationships with sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-Time-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Journalists who have attempted to understand Gamergate's motivations have concluded that, rather than relating to ethics, they are part of a culture war to suppress views with which Gamergate supporters disagree. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Verge" title="The Verge">The Verge</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Chris Plante wrote that under the guise of ethics concerns, Gamergate supporters repeatedly attacked him for criticizing mainstream video games from the point of view of his social convictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VergeDead_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-VergeDead-157">[157]</a></sup> <i>Columbia Journalism Review</i> writer Chris Ip said "many criticisms of press coverage by people who identify with Gamergate ... have been debunked" and concluded that "at core, the movement is a classic culture war".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-columbia_journalism_review_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-columbia_journalism_review-84">[84]</a></sup> Alyssa Rosenberg of <i>The Washington Post</i> said that some of the ostensible concerns about video game reviews are actually rooted in Gamergate supporters' belief that video games are appliances rather than art and that they should be reviewed based on feature checklists rather than traditional artistic criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPoRosenberg_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaPoRosenberg-158">[158]</a></sup> Chris Suellentrop of <i>The New York Times</i> criticized resistance to innovative uses of the gaming medium, and the belief that increased coverage and praise of artistic games like <i><a href="/wiki/Gone_Home" title="Gone Home">Gone Home</a></i> would negatively affect blockbuster games such as <i>Grand Theft Auto V</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTSuellentrop_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTSuellentrop-125">[125]</a></sup> After analyzing a sample of tweets related to Gamergate, <i>Newsweek</i> concluded that it was primarily about harassment rather than ethics, stating that the sample "suggests that ... contrary to its stated goal, Gamergate spends more time tweeting negatively at game developers than at game journalists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newsweek_Brandwatch_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Newsweek_Brandwatch-82">[82]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ars_Technica" title="Ars Technica">Ars Technica</a></i>, analyzing logs from the 4chan users who initially pushed Gamergate into the spotlight, wrote that the goal behind the hashtag campaign was to "perpetuate misogynistic attacks by wrapping them in a debate about ethics in gaming journalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ars_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ars-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gamergate has been criticized for focusing on women, especially female developers, while ignoring many large-scale journalistic ethics issues.
