Alex Goldman of NPR's <i>On the Media</i> criticized Gamergate for targeting female indie developers rather than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AAA_(game_industry)" title="AAA (game industry)">AAA games publishers</a>, and said claims of unethical behavior by Quinn and Sarkeesian were unfounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldman_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-goldman-8">[8]</a></sup> In <i>Wired</i>, Laura Hudson found it telling that Gamergate supporters concentrated on impoverished independent creators and critics, and nearly exclusively women, rather than the large game companies whose work they enjoyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WiredHudson_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-WiredHudson-149">[149]</a></sup> <i>Vox</i> writer Todd VanDerWerff highlighted an essay written by game developer David Hill, who said that corruption, <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a>, and excessive <a href="/wiki/Commercialism" title="Commercialism">commercialism</a> existed in the gaming industry, but that Gamergate was not addressing those issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VoxConfuse_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-VoxConfuse-107">[107]</a></sup> Adi Robertson, of <i>The Verge</i>, commented on the long-standing ethical issues gaming journalism has dealt with, but that most Gamergate supporters did not seem interested in "addressing problems that don't directly relate to feminist criticism or the tiny indie games scene".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VergeDay_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-VergeDay-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gamergate_activities">Gamergate activities</span></h2>
<p>Following the accusations against Quinn, proponents of Gamergate began to use the "KotakuInAction" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subreddits" title="Subreddits">subreddit</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Message_board" title="Message board">boards</a> on 8chan to discuss and organize. Because of its anonymous membership, lack of organization and leaderless nature, sources differ as to the goals or mission of Gamergate and, with no person or group able to speak for Gamergate, defining it has been difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYMag_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-NYMag-10">[10]</a></sup> As the threats expanded, international media focused on Gamergate's violent, misogynistic element and its inability to present a coherent message. Writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a>,</i> Bob Stuart reported that: "Gamergate has since swelled into an unwieldy movement with no apparent leaders, mission statement, or aims beyond calling out "social justice warriors". [...] When members of the games industry are being driven from their houses and jobs, threatened, or abused, it makes Gamergate's claim that it is engaged in an ethical campaign appear laughable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeleStuart_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-TeleStuart-9">[9]</a></sup> Reporting on Gamergate has also been made difficult by the intense harassment that some researchers have gotten when using the Gamergate hashtag, with some organizations advising people to not use the term online to avoid this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marysue_phd_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-marysue_phd-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jesse Singal, writing for <a href="/wiki/New_York_(magazine)" title="New York (magazine)"><i>New York</i></a> magazine based on a post he made to Reddit, stated that he had spoken to several Gamergate supporters to try to understand their concerns, but found conflicting ideals and incoherent messages. Singal observed Gamergate supporters making a constant series of attacks on Quinn, Sarkeesian, and other women, while frequently stating that Gamergate "is not about them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYMag_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-NYMag-10">[10]</a></sup> The <i>Columbia Journalism Review's</i> Chris Ip said any legitimate message from Gamergate supporters regarding ethics in journalism was being lost in the noise created by harassment, sexism, and misogyny. With anyone able to tweet under the hashtag and no single person willing or able to represent the hashtag and take responsibility for its actions, Ip said it is not possible for journalists to neatly separate abusers from those seeking reasonable debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-columbia_journalism_review_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-columbia_journalism_review-84">[84]</a></sup> Singal was critical of Gamergate's lack of organization and leadership commenting on their "refus[al] to appoint a leader or write up a platform".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYMag_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-NYMag-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> research fellow <a href="/wiki/Anders_Sandberg" title="Anders Sandberg">Anders Sandberg</a> observed that the Gamergate debate "has been a train wreck hard to look away from". He argues that the vituperative nature of the discourse is the result of its origins in imageboard subculture, which values anonymity and promotes the kind of mob behavior, where any publicly stated claim could justifiably result in a wave of hostility. Noting that those rules are "radically different" from other subcultures, the result was that "when the Chan culture touches other cultures of discourse there will be fundamental misunderstandings about the very nature of what a discourse is supposed to be."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandberg_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandberg-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ryan Cooper of <i>The Week</i> highlighted an analysis by writer Jon Stone: "[Gamergate] readjusts and reinvents itself in response to attempts to disarm and disperse its noxiousness, subsuming disaffected voices in an act of continual regeneration, cycling through targets, pretexts, manifestoes, and moralisms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryan_Cooper_Week_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ryan_Cooper_Week-99">[99]</a></sup> Christopher Grant, editor-in-chief of <i><a href="/wiki/Polygon_(website)" title="Polygon (website)">Polygon</a></i>, said that Gamergate has remained amorphous and leaderless so that the harassment can be conducted without any culpability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OTMGrant_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-OTMGrant-162">[162]</a></sup> Grant said that meant that "ultimately Gamergate will be defined—I think has been defined—by some of its basest elements".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-columbia_journalism_review_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-columbia_journalism_review-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over time, the Gamergate movement's focus has broadened from video games into an aggressive campaign against both the news media and what they call "social justice warriors," such as with <a href="/wiki/Bollea_v._Gawker" title="Bollea v. Gawker">Hulk Hogan's lawsuit against Gawker Media</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Efforts_to_impact_public_perceptions">Efforts to impact public perceptions</span></h3>
<p>Early in the controversy, posters on 4chan focused on donating to a self-described <a href="/wiki/Radical_feminism" title="Radical feminism">radical feminist</a> group called <a href="/wiki/The_Fine_Young_Capitalists" title="The Fine Young Capitalists">The Fine Young Capitalists</a> (TFYC), who had been embroiled in a dispute with Quinn over a female-only game development contest they had organized. Advocating donations to help TFYC create the game, posters on 4chan's politics board argued that such donations would make them "look really good" and would make them "PR-untouchable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vice_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vice-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastcodesign_2014-11-03_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-fastcodesign_2014-11-03-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APGN_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-APGN-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>To respond to widespread criticism of Gamergate as misogynistic, Gamergate supporters adopted a second Twitter hashtag, #NotYourShield, intended to show that some women and minorities in the gaming community were supportive of #Gamergate and critical of Quinn and Sarkeesian. In the 4chan post that <i>Ars Technica</i> said may have coined the hashtag, this was framed as a way to "demand the SJWs stop using you as a shield to deflect genuine criticism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ars_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ars-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeMonde_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeMonde-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Ars Technica</i> and <i>The Daily Dot</i> reported that a series of logs from 4chan chat rooms and discussion boards indicated that the #NotYourShield hashtag was created on 4chan. <i>Ars Technica</i> said that many of the avatars for accounts used to tweet the tag seemed to be <a href="/wiki/Sockpuppet_(Internet)" title="Sockpuppet (Internet)">sockpuppets</a> that had been copied from elsewhere on the internet, and compared the hashtag to #EndFathersDay, a hoax manufactured on 4chan using similar methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ars_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ars-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DailyDot_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-DailyDot-167">[167]</a></sup> Quinn said that in light of Gamergate's exclusive targeting of women or those who stood up for women, "#notyourshield was, ironically, solely designed to be a shield for this campaign once people started calling it misogynistic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Escapist4chan_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Escapist4chan-168">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Chu" title="Arthur Chu">Arthur Chu</a> wrote that the hashtag was an attempt to leverage <a href="/wiki/White_guilt" title="White guilt">white guilt</a> and to prevent allies from supporting the people being attacked by Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chu2014_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chu2014-169">[169]</a></sup> Members of 4chan have said that most of the information was taken out of context or misrepresented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Escapist4chan_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Escapist4chan-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2015 <a href="/wiki/Tim_Schafer" title="Tim Schafer">Tim Schafer</a> mocked the #NotYourShield campaign during a speech at a gaming convention, referencing the belief that it was largely composed of sockpuppets, and was immediately faced with a barrage of harassment and abuse. A campaign dubbed '#SchaferSocks' followed soon after, raising money for individuals in need of socks. The outraged response to Schafer's remarks from within Gamergate was mocked by many of its opponents, who tweeted support for Schafer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaferProfile_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchaferProfile-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BelfastTelegraph_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-BelfastTelegraph-171">[171]</a></sup> In a later interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Polygon_(website)" title="Polygon (website)">Polygon</a></i>, Schafer clarified that he saw Gamergate as using #NotYourShield as a shield against criticism. He added while he recognized some may be using the tag for legitimate reasons, he felt "it exposed a lot of the ridiculousness of [Gamergate] through the overstated reaction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polygon_2015-05-20_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-polygon_2015-05-20-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Targeting_advertisers">Targeting advertisers</span></h3>
<p>Gamergate supporters were critical of the wave of articles calling for diversity that followed the initial outbreak of the controversy; interpreting them as an attack on games and gamer culture. Gamergaters responded with a coordinated email campaign that demanded advertisers drop several involved publications; in a five-step 'war plan' against organizations that offended them, a Gamergate posting described how they would choose from a list of target organizations, pick a grievance from a list others had compiled, and send a form letter containing it to an advertiser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waposuccess_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-waposuccess-173">[173]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> reacted to this by withdrawing an ad campaign from Gamasutra in October 2014. After a number of game developers criticized Intel for this, arguing that it could have a chilling effect on free speech and that it amounted to supporting harassment, Intel apologized for appearing to take sides in the controversy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIBizIntel_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIBizIntel-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VergeIntelStatement_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-VergeIntelStatement-175">[175]</a></sup> and resumed advertising on Gamasutra in mid-November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Intel_Telegraph_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Intel_Telegraph-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>In mid-October 2014, Sam Biddle, an editor for the Gawker blog <i><a href="/wiki/Valleywag" title="Valleywag">Valleywag</a></i>, made a series of derisive tweets that stated that "[u]ltimately #Gamergate is reaffirming what we've known to be true for decades: nerds should be constantly shamed and degraded into submission" and "Bring Back Bullying".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cjr_gawker_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-cjr_gawker-177">[177]</a></sup> In response, <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> temporarily pulled advertising from Gawker and <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Systems" title="Adobe Systems">Adobe Systems</a> requested that Gawker remove its logo from Gawker's advertising page while stating that it "stands against bullying".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waposuccess_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-waposuccess-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelegraphDyson_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-TelegraphDyson-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biadobe_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-biadobe-179">[179]</a></sup> Adobe later clarified that it had never been a Gawker advertiser and explicitly disowned Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AdobeClarify_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-AdobeClarify-180">[180]</a></sup> Gawker reported losing thousands of dollars as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biadobe_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-biadobe-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaxRead_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaxRead-181">[181]</a></sup> Biddle later stated that the tweets were jokes, and apologized for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waposuccess_173-2"><a href="#cite_note-waposuccess-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cjr_gawker_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-cjr_gawker-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adobe_recode_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-adobe_recode-182">[182]</a></sup> Commenting on the actions of Intel and Adobe and the public response, trade publication <i><a href="/wiki/Advertising_Age" title="Advertising Age">Advertising Age</a></i> warned advertisers that responding to Gamergate was a "<a href="/wiki/No-win_situation" title="No-win situation">lose-lose situation</a>", and that brands "not responding are in better shape than those who have".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adage_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adage-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>In an attempt to further damage Gawker's income, in late October some Gamergate supporters initiated "Operation Baby Seal". This campaign aimed at removing <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Google_AdSense" title="Google AdSense">AdSense</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" title="Amazon.com">Amazon</a>'s Associates advertising platforms from Gawker by mass-reporting apparent violations of the ad agencies' <a href="/wiki/Terms_of_service" title="Terms of service">terms of service</a> in Gawker's published content. <i>Vox</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s VanDerWerff identified that while efforts to convince advertisers to pull ads is not new in the history of journalism, this new tactic of targeting the ad providers is on a grander scale and has the potential, if successful, to financially harm Gawker. He said that with the campaign Gamergate seemed less interested in exposing ethical lapses, and more concerned with shuttering sites it does not agree with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-op_baby_seal_vox_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-op_baby_seal_vox-184">[184]</a></sup> The name is based on a <i><a href="/wiki/Wondermark" title="Wondermark">Wondermark</a></i> webcomic parodying Gamergate supporters, causing them to sometimes be nicknamed 'sealions'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boingboing_chanculture_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-boingboing_chanculture-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Terrible_Sea_Lion_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Terrible_Sea_Lion-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea_Lion_Has_Been_Verbed_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sea_Lion_Has_Been_Verbed-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_errata_Wondermark_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-2014_errata_Wondermark-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaming_industry_response">Gaming industry response</span></h2>
<p>The harassment of Quinn, Sarkeesian, Wu, and others led prominent industry professionals to condemn the Gamergate attacks for damaging the video gaming community and the public perception of the industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Straumsheim2015_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Straumsheim2015-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsweek_2015-05-31_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-newsweek_2015-05-31-189">[189]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(magazine)" title="Vanity Fair (magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Laura Parker stated that the Gamergate situation led those outside of the video game industry to be "flooded with evidence of the video-game community as a poisonous and unwelcoming place", furthering any negative views they may have had of video games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vanityfair_2015-08-04_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-vanityfair_2015-08-04-190">[190]</a></sup> Independent game developer Andreas Zecher wrote an open letter calling upon the community to take a stand against the attacks, attracting the signatures of more than two thousand professionals within the gaming industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-105">[105]</a></sup> Many in the industry saw the signatures "as proof that the people sending vicious attacks at Quinn and Sarkeesian weren't representative of the video game industry overall".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-191">[191]</a></sup> Writing for <i>The Guardian</i>, Jenn Frank described the tactics used in the harassment campaign and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_of_fear" title="Climate of fear">climate of fear</a> it generated through its attacks on women and their allies, concluding that this alienating and abusive environment would harm not only women but also the industry as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardianJF_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuardianJF-192">[192]</a></sup> Frank herself received significant harassment for writing this article, and announced an intention to quit games journalism as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vox_87-5"><a href="#cite_note-Vox-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slate_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-slate-115">[115]</a></sup> Games designer Damion Schubert said that Gamergate was "an unprecedented catastrof**k [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>]", and that silencing critiques of games harms games developers by depriving them of feedback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EscapistSubertInterview_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-EscapistSubertInterview-193">[193]</a></sup> Several video game developers, journalists, and gamers from across various gender, racial, and social backgrounds adopted new Twitter hashtags, such as #INeedDiverseGames, #StopGamergate2014 and #GamersAgainstGamergate, to show solidarity with the people targeted by the harassment and their opposition to the reactionary messages from Gamergate supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salon_2014-12-23_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-salon_2014-12-23-194">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans.2C_Janish_2015_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans.2C_Janish_2015-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Frontier_Foundation" title="Electronic Frontier Foundation">Electronic Frontier Foundation</a> characterized Gamergate as a "magnet for harassment", and notes the possible financial risk for companies dealing with it on social media platforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eff_2015-01-08_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-eff_2015-01-08-196">[196]</a></sup> The Entertainment Software Association (ESA) issued a statement condemning the harassment, stating that "[t]here is no place in the video game community—or our society—for personal attacks and threats".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapost_esa_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapost_esa-197">[197]</a></sup> ESA president Mike Gallagher, speaking at the June 2015 Electronic Entertainment Expo, clarified that the ESA did not become more involved as they felt it was an argument that was outside their industry and their involvement would have been disruptive, but praised the efforts to counter harassment that will benefit the industry in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gamasutra_2015-06-14_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-gamasutra_2015-06-14-198">[198]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/BlizzCon" title="BlizzCon">BlizzCon</a> 2014, <a href="/wiki/Blizzard_Entertainment" title="Blizzard Entertainment">Blizzard Entertainment</a> president and co-founder <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mike_Morhaime" title="Mike Morhaime">Mike Morhaime</a> denounced recent harassment; blaming a "small group of people [who] have been doing really awful things" and "tarnishing our reputation" as gamers. He called on attendees to treat each other with kindness and demonstrate to the world that the community rejects harassment. His statements were widely interpreted as referring to Gamergate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNet_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNet-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joystiqblizzcon_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-joystiqblizzcon-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCVUK_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCVUK-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCGamerMM_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCGamerMM-202">[202]</a></sup> CEOs of both the American and European branches of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sony_Computer_Entertainment" title="Sony Computer Entertainment">Sony Computer Entertainment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shawn_Layden" title="Shawn Layden">Shawn Layden</a> and Jim Ryan respectively, said the harassment and bullying were absolutely horrific and that such inappropriate behavior would not be tolerated at Sony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ibtimes_2015-02-23_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-ibtimes_2015-02-23-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sony_response_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-sony_response-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venture_sony_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-venture_sony-205">[205]</a></sup> The Swedish Games Industry issued a statement denouncing the harassment and sexism from Gamergate supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GuardianQvist_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-GuardianQvist-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2015, Quinn and Alex Lifschitz created the <a href="/wiki/Crash_Override_Network" title="Crash Override Network">Crash Override Network</a>, a private group of experts who provide free support and counsel to those that have been harassed online, including as a result of Gamergate, and to work with law authorities and social media sites in response to such threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSM_2015-01-20_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSM_2015-01-20-207">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verge_2015-01-17_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verge_2015-01-17-208">[208]</a></sup> The Crash Override Network has aligned its activities with the <a href="/wiki/Online_Abuse_Prevention_Initiative" title="Online Abuse Prevention Initiative">Online Abuse Prevention Initiative</a>, a non-profit organization started by software developer Randi Harper, that also seeks to provide aid to those harassed online.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marysue_2015-05-16_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-marysue_2015-05-16-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>Responses to Gamergate have encouraged the video game industry to review its treatment of women and minorities, and to make changes to support them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo_3_women_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo_3_women-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slate_GG2014_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slate_GG2014-211">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nightline_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-nightline-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fortune_august2015_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-fortune_august2015-213">[213]</a></sup> Intel, following its accidental involvement in Gamergate, pledged more than $300 million to help support a "Diversity in Technology" program with partners including Sarkeesian's Feminist Frequency organization and the IGDA, aimed at increasing the number of women and minorities in the industry. Intel CEO <a href="/wiki/Brian_Krzanich" title="Brian Krzanich">Brian Krzanich</a> stated in announcing the program that "it's not good enough to say we value diversity, and then have our industry not fully represent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polygon_Intel_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polygon_Intel-214">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes_Intel_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes_Intel-215">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_2015-01-07_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired_2015-01-07-216">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Arts" title="Electronic Arts">Electronic Arts</a> (EA) COO <a href="/wiki/Peter_Moore_(businessman)" title="Peter Moore (businessman)">Peter Moore</a> said the controversy made EA pay more attention to diversity and inclusion, telling <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> "[i]f there's been any benefit to Gamergate, [...] I think it just makes us think twice at times".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fortune_2015-09-04_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-fortune_2015-09-04-217">[217]</a></sup> Speaking about Gamergate harassment to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seattle_Times" title="Seattle Times">Seattle Times</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IGDA" title="IGDA">IGDA</a> executive director Kate Edwards said, "Gaming culture has been pretty misogynistic for a long time now. There's ample evidence of that over and over again... What we're finally seeing is that it became so egregious that now companies are starting to wake up and say, "We need to stop this. This has got to change."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to a perceived <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict of interest</a> between game developers and journalists, <i>Kotaku</i> and <i>Polygon</i> adopted policies of prohibiting or disclosing <a href="/wiki/Patreon" title="Patreon">Patreon</a> contributions to game developers respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pcmag_about_gg_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-pcmag_about_gg-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CathyYoung_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-CathyYoung-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Electronic Entertainment Expo 2015, which is used by the major video game publishers to reveal new titles in development, included markedly more female protagonists in these new games, as well as more visible presence by women at the event overall. Some commentators characterized this as a response to Gamergate and a rejection of the misogynistic harassment Gamergate had perpetrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RepresentationStrides_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-RepresentationStrides-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GamergateFail_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-GamergateFail-220">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BadassCharacters_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-BadassCharacters-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBTBatman_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBTBatman-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>The game <i><a href="/wiki/Batman:_Arkham_Knight" title="Batman: Arkham Knight">Batman: Arkham Knight</a></i> references Gamergate with hashtag, #CrusaderGate, which the <a href="/wiki/Riddler" title="Riddler">Riddler</a> used to unsuccessfully try to rally the Internet against Batman; bemoaning its failure, the Riddler describes those who use the hashtag as "idiotic and easily roused rabble".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArkhamKnightMcVuk_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArkhamKnightMcVuk-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wider_reception">Wider reception</span></h2>
<p>Anita Sarkeesian was named as one of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine's list of the 30 most influential people on the Internet in March 2015, and later in the magazine's Top 100 Most Influential People of 2015, citing her role in highlighting sexism in the video game community in the wake of the Gamergate controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_2015-03-05_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-time_2015-03-05-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_2015-04-16_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-time_2015-04-16-225">[225]</a></sup> She was also highlighted as one of <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitan_(magazine)" title="Cosmopolitan (magazine)">Cosmopolitan</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s fifty "Internet's Most Fascinating" in a 2015 list due to her efforts to curb online harassment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmo_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-cosmo-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Law_%26_Order:_Special_Victims_Unit_(season_16)#Episodes" title="Law &amp; Order: Special Victims Unit (season 16)">Intimidation Game</a>", an episode of the American crime drama television series <i><a href="/wiki/Law_%26_Order:_Special_Victims_Unit" title="Law &amp; Order: Special Victims Unit">Law &amp; Order: Special Victims Unit</a></i>, first broadcast on February 11, 2015, portrays a fictionalized version of the Gamergate controversy, including a character some observers said resembled Sarkeesian and whose story seemed based on those of women involved in the controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ars_2015-02-12_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-ars_2015-02-12-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge_2015-02-12_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge_2015-02-12-227">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-observer_2015-02-12_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-observer_2015-02-12-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-washingtonpost_2015-02-12_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-washingtonpost_2015-02-12-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the run up to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puppygate" title="Puppygate">2015 Hugo Awards</a> for <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> writing, organized groups <a href="/wiki/Voting_bloc" title="Voting bloc">voting in bloc</a> (the "<a href="/wiki/Sad_Puppies" title="Sad Puppies">Sad Puppies</a>" and a Gamergate-affiliated splinter group led by <a href="/wiki/Vox_Day" title="Vox Day">Vox Day</a>, calling themselves "Rabid Puppies") completely filled the nominations for five categories with white male authors who wrote action-oriented stories without social commentary. The <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Wired_(website)" title="Wired (website)">Wired</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i>, and other reports described the campaign as a backlash against the increasing racial, ethnic, and gender diversity in science fiction, while members of the bloc gave a variety of reasons for their actions, saying that they sought to counteract what they saw as a focus on giving awards based on the race, ethnicity, or gender of the author or characters rather than quality, and bemoaning the increasing prominence of what they described as 'message' fiction with fewer traditional "zap gun" sci-fi trappings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atlantic_2015-04-09_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-atlantic_2015-04-09-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bostonglobe_2015-04-07_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-bostonglobe_2015-04-07-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-latimes_2015-08-24_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-latimes_2015-08-24-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_2015-08-23_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired_2015-08-23-234">[234]</a></sup> Ultimately, vote tallies suggest around 19% of voters followed these groups' suggestions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hugoanalysis_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-hugoanalysis-235">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2015_Hugo_Award_Statistics_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-2015_Hugo_Award_Statistics-236">[236]</a></sup> though except <i><a href="/wiki/Guardians_of_the_Galaxy_(film)" title="Guardians of the Galaxy (film)">Guardians of the Galaxy</a></i>, none of the works nominated by these groups won an award. Convention members preferred "no award" to any nominee in the five categories filled by these groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2015_Hugo_Winners_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-2015_Hugo_Winners-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wired_2015-08-23_1_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-wired_2015-08-23_1-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2015 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/GTFO_(film)" title="GTFO (film)">GTFO</a></i> analyzed issues of sexism and harassment in video gaming. Already in production prior to the events of Gamergate the film's director, Shannon Sun-Higginson, addressed the controversy in a post-script to the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_2015-03-13_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes_2015-03-13-239">[239]</a></sup> Sun-Higginson stated Gamergate was "a terrible, terrible thing, but it's actually symptomatic of a wider, cultural, systemic problem".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_2015-03-08_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_2015-03-08-240">[240]</a></sup> The Gamergate situation was covered as part of a larger topic of online harassment towards women in the June 21, 2015, episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Week_Tonight_with_John_Oliver" title="Last Week Tonight with John Oliver">Last Week Tonight with John Oliver</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_2015-06-22_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-time_2015-06-22-241">[241]</a></sup> while the impact of the Gamergate controversy on Brianna Wu was the subject of the March 16, 2016, episode of the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Internet_Ruined_My_Life" title="The Internet Ruined My Life">The Internet Ruined My Life</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnnmoney_2016-03-16_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnnmoney_2016-03-16-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>An on-line abuse panel (itself the subject of controversy) at the 2016 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SXSW" title="SXSW">SXSW</a> festival said that there was no technological solution to the problem of harassment given human nature;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SXSW_On-Line_Harassment_Panel_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-SXSW_On-Line_Harassment_Panel-243">[243]</a></sup> although policy changes have been made, the larger issue is more societal than platform-specific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_societal_Issue_vis-.C3.A0-vis_a_platform_issue._244-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_societal_Issue_vis-.C3.A0-vis_a_platform_issue.-244">[244]</a></sup> Also, the sheer volume of the material to be vetted was described as being problematic by the corporations involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SXSW_Internet_Hate_Forum_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-SXSW_Internet_Hate_Forum-245">[245]</a></sup></p>
<p>Referring to this discussion in a speech for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_History_Month" title="Women's History Month">Women's History Month</a>, <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> said that "We know that women gamers face harassment and stalking and threats of violence from other players. When they speak out about their experiences, they're attacked on Twitter and other social media outlets, even threatened in their homes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obama_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-obama-246">[246]</a></sup> Obama urged targets of harassment to speak out, praising the courage of those who had resisted online harassment. "And what's brought these issues to light is that there are a lot of women out there, especially young women, who are speaking out bravely about their experiences, even when they know they'll be attacked for it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obama_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-obama-246">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dailydot_2016-03-18_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-dailydot_2016-03-18-247">[247]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gamergate has since become a notable cultural component of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Alt-right" title="Alt-right">alt-right</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2016_American_presidential_election" title="2016 American presidential election">2016 American presidential election</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alt-right_precursor_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alt-right_precursor-248">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-From_Video_Games_to_Politics_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-From_Video_Games_to_Politics-249">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Old_Racism.2C_New_Technology_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Racism.2C_New_Technology-250">[250]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_media">Social media</span></h3>
<p>Twitter was criticized for its inability to respond quickly and prevent harassment over the service. Within the United States, Twitter and other social media sites are not liable for content posted by third-parties of their service under <a href="/wiki/Section_230_of_the_Communications_Decency_Act" title="Section 230 of the Communications Decency Act">Section 230 of the Communications Decency Act</a> (1996), and this removes any legal pressure for these sites to police malicious content such as harassment and threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastcompany_2015-06-02_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-fastcompany_2015-06-02-134">[134]</a></sup> Brianna Wu, shortly after becoming a target of harassment, stated that Twitter facilitated harassment by the ease with which anyone could make a new account even after having an earlier account blocked, and challenged the service to improve its responsiveness to complaints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweek_wu_twitter_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek_wu_twitter-251">[251]</a></sup> Robinson Meyer of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i> said Gamergate is an "identity crisis" for Twitter, as, by not dealing with harassing users as Facebook has, the platform is failing to protect victims and losing readers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atlantic_twitter_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-atlantic_twitter-252">[252]</a></sup> Early in the Gamergate controversy, software developer Randi Harper started the "Good Game Auto Blocker" or "ggautoblocker", an expanding list of known Twitter accounts that were tied to the Gamergate hashtag which could be automatically <a href="/wiki/Block_(Internet)" title="Block (Internet)">blocked</a>, therefore reducing the degree of harassment received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge_2015-06-10_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge_2015-06-10-253">[253]</a></sup> In November 2014, Twitter announced a collaboration with the non-profit group "Women, Action &amp; the Media" (WAM), in which users of Twitter can report harassment to a tool monitored by WAM members, who would forward affirmed issues to Twitter within 24 hours. The move, while in the wake of the Gamergate harassment, was due to general issues of the harassment of women on the Internet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_twitter_wam_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj_twitter_wam-254">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wam_release_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-wam_release-255">[255]</a></sup> The report, released in May 2015, determined that of 512 reported harassment instances by the tool during the month of November 2014, 12% of those were tied to the Gamergate controversy based on the ggautoblocker list, with most harassment occurring from single-instance accounts targeting a single person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wam_report_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-wam_report-256">[256]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-wired_2015-08-23-234"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-wired_2015-08-23_234-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Wallace, Amy (August 23, 2015). <a class="external text" href="http://www.wired.com/2015/08/won-science-fictions-hugo-awards-matters/" rel="nofollow">"Who Won Science Fiction's Hugo Awards, and Why It Matters"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 3,</span> 2015</span>. <q>Brad Torgersen, one of the Sad Puppy leaders told me ... "the cognitive dissonance of people saying, 'No, the Hugos are about quality,' and then at the same time they're like: 'Ooh, we can vote for this author because they're gay, or for this story because it's got gay characters,' or, 'Ooh, we're going to vote for this author because they're not white.' As soon as that becomes the criteria, well, quality goes out the window." ... "When people go on about how we're anti-diversity, I'm like: No. All we're saying is storytelling ought to come first."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGamergate+controversy&amp;rft.aufirst=Amy&amp;rft.aulast=Wallace&amp;rft.btitle=Who+Won+Science+Fiction%27s+Hugo+Awards%2C+and+Why+It+Matters&amp;rft.date=2015-08-23&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.wired.com%2F2015%2F08%2Fwon-science-fictions-hugo-awards-matters%2F&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
